<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The Gonium pectorale genome demonstrates co-option of cell cycle regulation during the evolution of multicellularity"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2016 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-04-22"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The transition to multicellularity has occurred numerous times in all domains of life, yet its initial steps are poorly understood. The volvocine green algae are a tractable system for understanding the genetic basis of multicellularity including the initial formation of cooperative cell groups. Here we report the genome sequence of the undifferentiated colonial alga, Gonium pectorale, where group formation evolved by co-option of the retinoblastoma cell cycle regulatory pathway. Significantly, expression of the Gonium retinoblastoma cell cycle regulator in unicellular Chlamydomonas causes it to become colonial. The presence of these changes in undifferentiated Gonium indicates extensive group-level adaptation during the initial step in the evolution of multicellularity. These results emphasize an early and formative step in the evolution of multicellularity, the evolution of cell cycle regulation, one that may shed light on the evolutionary history of other multicellular innovations and evolutionary transitions. The undifferentiated Gonium pectorale represents the initial transition to multicellularity. Here, Bradley Olson, Erik Hanschen and colleagues describe the genome of Gonium pectorale, demonstrating that co-option of the retinoblastoma cell cycle regulatory pathway was a key genetic change in the evolution of multicellularity.
                  
                    
                  
                "/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The transition to multicellularity has occurred numerous times in all domains of life, yet its initial steps are poorly understood. The volvocine green algae are a tractable system for understanding the genetic basis of multicellularity including the initial formation of cooperative cell groups. Here we report the genome sequence of the undifferentiated colonial alga, <i>Gonium pectorale,</i> where group formation evolved by co-option of the retinoblastoma cell cycle regulatory pathway. Significantly, expression of the <i>Gonium</i> retinoblastoma cell cycle regulator in unicellular <i>Chlamydomonas</i> causes it to become colonial. The presence of these changes in undifferentiated <i>Gonium</i> indicates extensive group-level adaptation during the initial step in the evolution of multicellularity. These results emphasize an early and formative step in the evolution of multicellularity, the evolution of cell cycle regulation, one that may shed light on the evolutionary history of other multicellular innovations and evolutionary transitions.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Multicellular organisms have independently evolved numerous times throughout the tree of life including plants, animals, fungi, cyanobacteria, amoeba, brown algae, red algae and green algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bonner, J. T. The origins of multicellularity. Integr. Biol. 1, 27â€“36 (1998) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3334e924">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Grosberg, R. K. &amp; Strathmann, R. R. The evolution of multicellularity: a minor major transition? Annu. Rev. Ecol. Evol. Syst. 38, 621â€“654 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3334e927">2</a></sup>. In animals, multicellularity emerged 600â€“950 million years ago (Myr ago) correlating with a large expansion of genes encoding transcription factors, signalling pathways, and cell adhesion genes that were co-opted from their unicellular ancestors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Suga, H. et al. The Capsaspora genome reveals a complex unicellular prehistory of animals. Nat. Commun. 4, 2325â€“2333 (2013) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3334e931">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="King, N. et al. The genome of the choanoflagellate Monosiga brevicollis and the origin of metazoans. Nature 451, 783â€“788 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3334e934">4</a></sup> Similarly, multicellular, terrestrial plants emerged <span class="stix">âˆ¼</span>750â€‰Myr ago correlating with an expansion of many signalling pathways present in their unicellular relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Parfrey, L., Lahr, D. J. G., Knoll, A. H. &amp; Katz, L. A. Estimating the timing of early eukaryotic diversification with multigene molecular clocks. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 13624â€“13629 (2011) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3334e938">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 1â€“9 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3334e941">6</a></sup>. However, because most multicellular lineages have long diverged from their unicellular relatives, the genomic signature of the transition to multicellularity has been obscured, and consequently this evolutionary process remains enigmatic.</p><p>The volvocine green algae are a unique model system for the evolution of multicellularity because the unicellular ancestry is clear, the emergence of multicellularity occurred <span class="stix">âˆ¼</span>230â€‰Myr ago, and species exhibit a stepwise increase in morphological complexity ranging from undifferentiated colonies to differentiated multicellular species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Herron, M. D., Hackett, J. D., Aylward, F. O. &amp; Michod, R. E. Triassic origin and early radiation of multicellular volvocine algae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 3254â€“3258 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3334e948">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kirk, D. L. Volvox: Molecular-Genetic Origins of Multicellularity and Cellular Differentiation Cambridge Univ. Press (1998) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3334e951">8</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Figs 1,2</a>). Unicellular <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> is thought to resemble the unicellular ancestor of multicellular volvocines, including undifferentiated <i>Gonium pectorale</i> and differentiated <i>Volvox carteri</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Volvocine phylogenetic tree."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Volvocine phylogenetic tree.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="347"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Evolution of cell cycle control (C), expanded ECM (E) and somatic cells (S) are denoted. (<b>b</b>) Micrographs of <i>Chlamydomonas</i> (green; scale bar, 10â€‰Î¼m), <i>Gonium</i> (blue; scale bar, 10â€‰Î¼m) and <i>Volvox</i> (black; scale bar, 25â€‰Î¼m) show morphological differences.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Chlamydomonas</i> undergoes a variant cell cycle (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), regulated by homologues of the retinoblastoma cell cycle pathway, termed multiple-fission where it divides by a series of rapid cell divisions producing individual daughter cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Goodenough, U. W. Control of cell division by a retinoblastoma protein homolog in Chlamydomonas. Genes Dev. 15, 1652â€“1661 (2001) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1017">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Fang, S.-C., de los Reyes, C. &amp; Umen, J. G. Cell size checkpoint control by the retinoblastoma tumor suppressor pathway. PLoS Genet. 2, e167 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1020">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1023">11</a></sup>. <i>Gonium</i> typically forms 8- or 16-celled undifferentiated colonies, where each constituent cell resembles a <i>Chlamydomonas</i> cell (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). <i>Gonium</i> also undergoes multiple fission forming daughter colonies by keeping cells attached after multiple-fission, suggesting either cell cycle regulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Shelton, D. E. &amp; Michod, R. E. Group selection and group adaptation during a major evolutionary transition: insights from the evolution of multicellularity in the volvocine algae. Biol. Theory 9, 452â€“469 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1040">12</a></sup>, or cellâ€“cell adhesion has been modified to promote multicellularity. In <i>Gonium</i>, like <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, growth and cell division are uncoupled<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Harper, J. D. I. &amp; John, P. C. L. Coordination of division events in the Chlamydomonas cell cycle. Protoplasma 131, 118â€“130 (1986) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1050">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Olson, B. J. S. C. in Genomic Insights Into the Biology of Algae Vol. 64, ed. Piganeau G. 185â€“243Elsevier (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1053">14</a></sup>. Asexual juvenile <i>Gonium</i> colonies grow (without cell division) into adults. After cell division through multiple-fission, juvenile colonies hatch forming 8 or 16 daughter colonies of 8 or 16 cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). <i>Volvox</i> contains approximately 2,000 small, terminally differentiated, somatic cells on the surface of the spheroid and approximately 16 large reproductive cells embedded in extracellular matrix (ECM) inside the spheroid (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). <i>Volvox</i> also has modified multiple fission where germâ€“soma separation is established after an asymmetric cell division<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kirk, D. L. Volvox: Molecular-Genetic Origins of Multicellularity and Cellular Differentiation Cambridge Univ. Press (1998) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1073">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Olson, B. J. S. C. in Genomic Insights Into the Biology of Algae Vol. 64, ed. Piganeau G. 185â€“243Elsevier (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1076">14</a></sup>.</p><p>The transition to multicellularity in the Volvocales was thought to involve at least 12 steps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kirk, D. L. A twelve-step program for evolving multicellularity and a division of labor. Bioessays 27, 299â€“310 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1086">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Herron, M. D. &amp; Michod, R. E. Evolution of complexity in the volvocine algae: transitions in individuality through Darwinâ€™s eye. Evolution 62, 436â€“451 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1089">16</a></sup> though the genetic basis of these steps remains enigmatic. Genomic comparison of the extremes of morphological complexity, <i>Chlamydomonas</i> and <i>Volvox</i>, suggests few genetic changes are required<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â€“226 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1099">17</a></sup>, but it is unclear how and when the genes important for multicellularity evolved during these 12 steps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Olson, B. J. S. C. in Genomic Insights Into the Biology of Algae Vol. 64, ed. Piganeau G. 185â€“243Elsevier (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1104">14</a></sup>.</p><p>By sequencing the genome of the undifferentiated, chlorophycean <i>Gonium pectorale</i>, a species without a differentiated ancestor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Herron, M. D. &amp; Michod, R. E. Evolution of complexity in the volvocine algae: transitions in individuality through Darwinâ€™s eye. Evolution 62, 436â€“451 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1115">16</a></sup>, we find co-option of cell cycle regulation, which occurred during the initial transition to cell groups, as the genetic basis for the evolution of multicellularity. The cell cycle regulation found in undifferentiated <i>Gonium</i>, co-opted in a multicellular context and shared with germâ€“soma-differentiated <i>Volvox</i>, indicates group-level adaptations in undifferentiated colonies. The early co-option of cell cycle regulation for group-level life cycle and reproduction is a critical and formative step in the evolution of multicellularity.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genomic comparisons of volvocine algae</h3><p>At the genomic level, the genomes of <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> are similar, though various measures of genome compactness correlate with cell number, consistent with a long-term increase in organismal size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The origins of genome complexity. Science 302, 1401â€“1404 (2003) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1146">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Smith, D. R. et al. Organelle genome complexity scales positively with organism size in volvocine green algae. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 793â€“797 (2013) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1149">19</a></sup>. <i>Chlamydomonas</i> and <i>Gonium</i> have similar GC content near 64%, while <i>Volvox</i> has 56% GC (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Otherwise, <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> have decreasing gene densities of 159.6, 120.9 and 113.7 genes per megabase, with an increasing average intron length of 279, 349 and 500 base pairs, respectively. Although intron length increases with organismal complexity, the number of introns per gene (<i>Chlamydomonas,</i> 7.46; <i>Gonium</i>, 6.5; <i>Volvox</i> 6.8, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Tab1">Table 1</a>) does not. GC content, intron length and gene density correlate with morphological complexity.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary statistics for genome level analyses for <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ncomms11370/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We next examined genome-wide evolution in all three species to better understand the genetic basis for the evolution of multicellularity. A prediction of lineage-specific genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Domazet-Loso, T., Brajkovic, J. &amp; Tautz, D. A phylostratigraphy approach to uncover the genomic history of major adaptations in metazoan lineages. Trends Genet. 23, 533â€“539 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1543">20</a></sup> shows few genes correlate with the evolution of multicellularity in the Volvocales (phylostratum 7; PS7) with a maximum of 180â€“357 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). This suggests that the evolution of multicellularity does not rely upon the evolution of <i>de novo</i> genes. Though gene regulation may be important during multicellular innovation, the diversity and abundance of transcription factors is similar in <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Table 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Although enrichment of transcription factors can correlate with the evolution of multicellularity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Suga, H. et al. The Capsaspora genome reveals a complex unicellular prehistory of animals. Nat. Commun. 4, 2325â€“2333 (2013) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1572">3</a></sup>, this is not always the case<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lang, D. et al. Genome-wide phylogenetic comparative analysis of plant transcriptional regulation: a timeline of loss, gain, expansion, and correlation with complexity. Genome Biol. Evol. 2, 488â€“503 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1576">21</a></sup>; we found that <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> have fewer transcription factors than in <i>Chlamydomonas</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). These include PHD domains (transcription and chromatin binding), DNA binding transcription factors and histones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Tables 2 and 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome similarity in the Volvocales."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genome similarity in the Volvocales.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="582"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Predicted number of genes in each phylostratum (PS1â€“PS9) for <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i>. (<b>b</b>) Heatmap of transcription factor abundance for all green algae. Significant over- (+) and under-representation (âˆ’) in colonial/multicellular lineages (<i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i>) is denoted (G test of independence, <i>Î±</i>=0.05). Rows are clustered (left), an accepted phylogeny is depicted (top). (<b>c</b>) Phylogenetic analysis of gene family evolution. Bars to the left and right of the vertical axis denote the lost and gained gene families respectively, relative to its parental node. (<b>d</b>) Venn diagram of the species distribution of Pfam A domains unique to the volvocine algae.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using all published chlorophyte green algae genomes, we constructed Markov-based gene families (<i>Chlamydomonas</i>, 73%; <i>Gonium</i>, 73%; <i>Volvox</i>, 70% of genes in gene families of size greater than one). Compared with 2,844 net gene families gained, which correlate with the origin of the Chlorophyceae, a phylogenetic analysis of these gene families suggests little protein innovation (110 net gene families) during the evolution of multicellularity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). These same green algae genomes allowed analysis into Pfam domain innovation, which may correlate with the evolution of multicellularity. We found innovation of only nine Pfam domains correlating with the evolution of multicellularity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Moreover, multicellular algae (<i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i>) have reduced Pfam domain diversity and abundance (compared with nine unicellular green algae, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Data 1</a>); 394 Pfam A domains are significantly under-represented versus 129 over-represented Pfam domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Interestingly, there is an excess of species-specific genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2a,c</a>) and Pfam domains (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>) compared with multicellularity-correlated genes and Pfam domains, suggesting that species-specific adaptations are more numerous than changes correlating with the evolution of multicellularity. We observe more evidence of species-specific, rather than multicellular-specific, protein innovations, suggesting species-specific adaptation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig2">Fig. 2a,c,d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Tables 5â€“8</a>) rather than genome-wide differences correlating with the evolution of multicellularity. The evolution of multicellularity in the volvocine algae does not require large-scale genomic innovation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Co-option of cell cycle regulation for multicellularity</h3><p>Notably, we observe that the genetic innovation correlating with multicellularity, shared between <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i>, evolved through co-option of existing developmental programs of cell cycle control. Volvocine algae have a common multiple-fission life cycle, with variation in timing and number of divisions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Olson, B. J. S. C. in Genomic Insights Into the Biology of Algae Vol. 64, ed. Piganeau G. 185â€“243Elsevier (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1718">14</a></sup>. Like most eukaryotes, including plants and animals, their cell cycles are regulated by homologues of the retinoblastoma cell cycle regulatory pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Figs 5â€“8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Table 9</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Goodenough, U. W. Control of cell division by a retinoblastoma protein homolog in Chlamydomonas. Genes Dev. 15, 1652â€“1661 (2001) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1732">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Fang, S.-C., de los Reyes, C. &amp; Umen, J. G. Cell size checkpoint control by the retinoblastoma tumor suppressor pathway. PLoS Genet. 2, e167 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1735">10</a></sup>, in which cyclin-dependent kinases (CDKs) bind cyclin proteins to phosphorylate and regulate retinoblastoma (<i>RB</i> or <i>MAT3</i> in the Volvocales), which in turn de-represses the cell cycle (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). Although most of these regulators are nearly identical in <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3b,e</a>), there are two notable differences. First, <i>Volvox</i> has a four gene expansion of cyclin D1 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â€“226 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1768">17</a></sup>. As <i>Volvox</i> has tissue differentiation, these cyclin D1 genes may have been important for tissue development as is the case in metazoans and land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Beumer, T. L., Roepers-Gajadien, H. L., Gademan, I. S., Kal, H. B. &amp; de Rooij, D. G. Involvement of the D-type cyclins in germ cell proliferation and differentiation in the mouse. Biol. Reprod. 63, 1893â€“1898 (2000) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1775">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Dewitte, W. et al. Altered cell cycle distribution, hyperplasia, and inhibited differentiation in Arabidopsis caused by the D-type cyclin CYCD3. Plant Cell 15, 79â€“92 (2003) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1778">23</a></sup>, supported by the fact that <i>RB</i> has moved into the mating locus of <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> and is differentially expressed between mating loci (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Ferris, P. J. et al. Evolution of an expanded sex-determining locus in Volvox. Science 328, 351â€“354 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1795">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hiraide, R. et al. The evolution of male-female sexual dimorphism predates the gender-based divergence of the mating locus gene MAT3/RB. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 1038â€“1040 (2013) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1798">25</a></sup>. However, the tandem array expansion of the cyclin D1 genes is also found in <i>Gonium</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>), where cyclin D genes display elevated dN/dS ratios compared with other cell cycle regulators (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>), suggesting the function of these cyclin Ds may be important for the transition to undifferentiated colonies, rather than tissue differentiation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Cell cycle pathway evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Cell cycle pathway evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="366"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The retinoblastoma cell cycle regulatory pathway. (<b>b</b>) Phylogeny of CDK genes. (<b>c</b>) Phylogeny of cyclin genes and syntenic relationships of cyclin D1 genes. (<b>d</b>) Phylogeny of <i>MAT3/RB</i> genes and comparison of <i>MAT3/RB</i> proteins. Domains RB-A and RB-B, the linker region in the binding pocket (L1), N-terminal conservation (N) and C-terminal conservation (C) are shown. Difference in shade of grey for <i>Volvox</i> male and <i>Volvox</i> female indicates sex-specific divergence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Ferris, P. J. et al. Evolution of an expanded sex-determining locus in Volvox. Science 328, 351â€“354 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1848">24</a></sup>. Conserved putative CDK phosphorylation sites are indicated with solid arrows, species-specific sites are indicated with open arrows. (<b>e</b>) E2F/DP1 genes. All trees have a midpoint root and bootstrap values above 80% are indicated.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Second, there is modification of the <i>RB</i> gene in <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Figs 5â€“7</a>). Protein dimers of cyclins and CDKs primarily regulate <i>RB</i> by phosphorylating serine or threonine residues, which is thought to regulate <i>RB</i> binding of chromatin via E2F/DP transcription factors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1888">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Khidr, L. &amp; Chen, P.-L. RB, the conductor that orchestrates life, death and differentiation. Oncogene 25, 5210â€“5219 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1891">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Narasimha, A. M. et al. Cyclin D activates the Rb tumor suppressor by mono-phosphorylation. Elife 3, 1â€“21 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1894">27</a></sup>. Recently it has been shown in human cells that cyclin D and CDK4/6 regulate monophosphorylation of <i>RB</i> proteins for G<sub>1</sub> phase-specific <i>RB</i> functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Narasimha, A. M. et al. Cyclin D activates the Rb tumor suppressor by mono-phosphorylation. Elife 3, 1â€“21 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1907">27</a></sup>. If similar in <i>Gonium</i>, this would suggest a role of the expanded cyclin D1 genes for regulating <i>RB</i> to express multicellularity-related genes during G<sub>1</sub> phase. If the expanded cyclin D1 proteins found in <i>Gonium</i> regulate multicellular cell cycle changes, modification of cyclin D-CDK phosphorylation sites in <i>RB</i> is predicted. Indeed, the linker of the E2F/DP binding pocket of <i>RB</i> is shorter in <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> compared with <i>Chlamydomonas</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Figs 5 and 6</a>), potentially altering how <i>RB</i> binds to chromatin via E2F/DP. In addition, phosphorylation sites between the E2F/DP pocket region (RB-A and RB-B domains) and the conserved carboxy (C)-terminal domain are absent in <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox RB</i> proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>). Interestingly, in animals the C terminus of <i>RB</i> is intertwined with E2F/DP and changes in the phosphorylation by cyclinâ€“CDK complexes could also alter E2F/DP binding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Rubin, S. M., Gall, A. L., Zheng, N. &amp; Pavletich, N. P. Structure of the Rb C-terminal domain bound to E2F1-DP1: a mechanism for phosphorylation-induced E2F release. Cell 123, 1093â€“1106 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3334e1960">28</a></sup>. As these phosphorylation sites are absent in <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox RB</i> proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>), this suggests that <i>RB</i> co-option for multicellularity may result in differences in locus-specific temporal expression of genes important for multicellularity during G<sub>1</sub>, such as cellâ€“cell adhesion genes. Given the role the <i>RB</i> pathway plays in regulating the cell cycle in <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, its early modification found in <i>Gonium</i>, and its co-option for complex morphology in <i>Volvox</i>, <i>RB</i> pathway regulation might be a key step towards multicellularity in the volvocine algae.</p><p>To test whether <i>RB</i> modifications present in <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> (compared with <i>Chlamydomonas</i>) are unrelated to, cause or are a consequence of multicellularity, we expressed the <i>Chlamydomonas</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2012">11</a></sup> and <i>Gonium RB</i> genes in a <i>Chlamydomonas</i> strain lacking its <i>RB</i> gene (<i>rb</i>, <i>mat3â€“4</i> strain, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Goodenough, U. W. Control of cell division by a retinoblastoma protein homolog in Chlamydomonas. Genes Dev. 15, 1652â€“1661 (2001) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2036">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2039">11</a></sup> using the promoter and terminator from the <i>Chlamydomonas RB</i> gene to ensure expression near wild-type levels (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">Fig. 4b,c</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2049">11</a></sup>. The <i>Chlamydomonas RB</i> gene rescues the small cell size defect in the <i>rb</i> mutant (<i>HA-CrRB::rb</i>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>), while the <i>Gonium RB</i> gene rescues the cell size defect and causes the <i>Chlamydomonas rb</i> mutant to become non-palmelloid colonial, ranging from 2 to 16 normal-sized cells (<i>HA-GpRB::rb</i>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). Crossing <i>RB</i> gain-of-function transformed <i>Chlamydomonas</i> strain to a <i>Chlamydomonas</i> strain lacking <i>DP1</i>, a gene that dimerizes with <i>E2F</i> to anchor <i>RB</i> to chromatin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2098">11</a></sup>, results in suppression of the colonial phenotype and large-sized cells (<i>HA-GpRB::rb::dp1</i>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>) consistent with the phenotype of the <i>Chlamydomonas dp1</i> mutant itself<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Fang, S.-C., de los Reyes, C. &amp; Umen, J. G. Cell size checkpoint control by the retinoblastoma tumor suppressor pathway. PLoS Genet. 2, e167 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2111">10</a></sup>. This demonstrates that the <i>Gonium RB</i> gene causes colonial multicellularity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>) through the <i>RB</i> pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>) and suggests differences in how <i>RB</i> binds chromatin and regulates the expression of cell cycle related genes in <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> are important for co-option of these <i>RB</i> targeted genes for multicellularity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Figs 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">4</a>). This gain-of-function demonstrates a causal link between cell cycle regulation and the group level during the evolution of multicellularity, emphasizing that multicellularity can evolve by co-option and modification of regulatory genes rather than extensive genomic differences or innovation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Gonium RB causes a colonial phenotype when expressed in Chlamydomonas."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: <i>Gonium RB</i> causes a colonial phenotype when expressed in <i>Chlamydomonas</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="439"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Transformation schematic showing resulting morphology overlaid onto cell and colony size measurements (logarithmic scale) of control <i>Chlamydomonas RB</i> mutant (<i>rb</i> or <i>mat3</i>â€“<i>4</i>, transformed with empty vector), complementing <i>HA-CrRB::rb</i> (two of five independent transformations are shown) and colonial <i>HA-GpRB::rb</i> (four independent transformations are shown). Crossing colonial <i>HA-GpRB::rb</i> to a <i>Chlamydomonas DP1</i> mutant (<i>dp1</i>) restores unicellularity in <i>Chlamydomonas</i> (one of two independent matings are shown). (<b>b</b>) Schematic <i>Gonium RB</i> tagged with 3XHA with its expression driven by the <i>Chlamydomonas RB</i> promoter and terminator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2209">11</a></sup>. (<b>c</b>) Anti-HA immunoblotting of <i>HA-CrRB::rb</i> and <i>HA-GpRB::rb</i> with anti-tubulin loading controls. Arrows indicate proteins at their expected molecular mass.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5"><i>Volvox</i> innovations for morphological complexity</h3><p>In <i>Volvox</i>, somatic differentiation is causally regulated by the <i>regA</i> gene cluster, a set of putative DNA-binding transcription factors thought to regulate chloroplast biogenesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Meissner, M., Stark, K., Cresnar, B., Kirk, D. L. &amp; Schmitt, R. Volvox germline-specific genes that are putative targets of RegA repression encode chloroplast proteins. Curr. Genet. 36, 363â€“370 (1999) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2248">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kirk, M. M. et al. regA, a Volvox gene that plays a central role in germ-soma differentiation, encodes a novel regulatory protein. Development 126, 639â€“647 (1999) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2251">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Duncan, L. et al. The VARL gene family and the evolutionary origins of the master cell-type regulatory gene, regA, in Volvox carteri. J. Mol. Evol. 65, 1â€“11 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2254">31</a></sup>. The <i>regA</i> gene cluster is absent in <i>Chlamydomonas</i> and <i>Gonium</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig5">Fig. 5a,b</a>), but is present in diverse <i>Volvox ferrisii</i> and <i>Volvox gigas</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hanschen, E. R., Ferris, P. J. &amp; Michod, R. E. Early evolution of the genetic basis for soma in the Volvocaceae. Evolution 68, 2014â€“2025 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2276">32</a></sup>, suggesting early evolution and co-option of this cluster shortly after the split of <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i> lineages (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hanschen, E. R., Ferris, P. J. &amp; Michod, R. E. Early evolution of the genetic basis for soma in the Volvocaceae. Evolution 68, 2014â€“2025 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2290">32</a></sup>. Interestingly, if the absence of <i>regA</i> in <i>Gonium</i> is indicative of the absence of <i>regA</i> in <i>Astrephomene</i>, with an independent evolution of somatic cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Herron, M. D. &amp; Michod, R. E. Evolution of complexity in the volvocine algae: transitions in individuality through Darwinâ€™s eye. Evolution 62, 436â€“451 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2310">16</a></sup>, <i>Astrephomene</i> may determine somatic cell fate through a different pathway than <i>Volvox</i> suggesting multiple evolutionary pathways and subsequent evolutionary consequences during the evolution of multicellularity. Indeed, undifferentiated multicellularity evolved once in the Volvocales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Herron, M. D. &amp; Michod, R. E. Evolution of complexity in the volvocine algae: transitions in individuality through Darwinâ€™s eye. Evolution 62, 436â€“451 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2320">16</a></sup>, while additional morphological complexity (for example, cellular differentiation and large <i>Volvox</i> body size) has repeatedly evolved, suggesting a relative ease to gain and lose additional complexity.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Genetic changes present in the Volvox genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Genetic changes present in the <i>Volvox</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="439"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Gene synteny near the <i>regA</i> gene cluster and closely related <i>regA</i>-like genes (bold). Chromosome or scaffold number is indicated. Conserved genes are linked by line segments for <i>regA</i>-like (thick, black) and neighbouring genes (thin, grey). (<b>b</b>) Phylogenetic relationships of <i>regA</i>-like genes. The tree is a midpoint root and bootstrap values above 70% are indicated. (<b>c</b>) Comparison of number of pherophorin and metalloprotease genes in <i>Chlamydomonas</i>, <i>Gonium</i> and <i>Volvox</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We investigated proteins related to morphological complexity, pherophorins and matrix metalloprotease (MMP) proteins, in the volvocine algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â€“226 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2386">17</a></sup>. These proteins are hypothesized to produce ECM and break up cell wall components during reproduction in <i>Gonium</i> and other Volvocales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kirk, D. L. A twelve-step program for evolving multicellularity and a division of labor. Bioessays 27, 299â€“310 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2393">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hallmann, A. Extracellular matrix and sex-inducing pheromone in Volvox. Int. Rev. Cytol. 227, 131â€“182 (2003) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2396">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Hallmann, A. The pherophorins: common, versatile building blocks in the evolution of extracellular matrix architecture in Volvocales. Plant J. 45, 292â€“307 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2399">34</a></sup>. While <i>Chlamydomonas</i> contains no ECM and <i>Gonium</i> contains little ECM, a <i>Volvox</i> spheroid is largely composed of ECM (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). Pherophorin and MMP gene families are expanded in <i>Volvox</i> relative to <i>Chlamydomonas</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â€“226 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2425">17</a></sup>. We found the expansion of pherophorins and MMP genes in <i>Volvox</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Data 2 and 3</a>) is not present in <i>Gonium</i>, though some species-specific expansion of MMP genes has occurred (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Data 2 and 3</a>). While some expansion of ECM gene families in <i>Gonium</i> was expected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Umen, J. G. &amp; Olson, B. J. S. C. in Genomic Insights Into the Biology of Algae Vol. 64, ed. Piganeau G. 185â€“243Elsevier (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2449">14</a></sup> to direct the cell wall layer synthesis of a <i>Gonium</i> colony, this layer may instead be directed through differential gene expression. Pherophorin, MMP expansion and cellular differentiation correlate with expanded organismal size rather than the origin of multicellularity, suggesting a subsequent step in the evolution of multicellularity.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>We have investigated the evolution of multicellularity in the volvocine algae by sequencing the genome of the undifferentiated <i>Gonium.</i> Despite morphological differences, it was known that the <i>Chlamydomonas</i> and <i>Volvox</i> genomes are strikingly similar, suggesting that multicellularity required few genetic innovations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Prochnik, S. E. et al. Genomic analysis of organismal complexity in the multicellular green alga Volvox carteri. Science 329, 223â€“226 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2474">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Merchant, S. S. et al. The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science 318, 245â€“250 (2007) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2477">35</a></sup>. However, these two genomes, positioned at the extremes of volvocine morphology, were unable to resolve the tempo and mode<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Simpson, G. G. Tempo and Mode in Evolution Columbia Univ. Press (1944) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2481">36</a></sup> of the evolutionary transition to multicellularity.</p><p>The evolution of multicellularity in the volvocine algae is thought to involve 12 morphological innovations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#MOESM570">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kirk, D. L. A twelve-step program for evolving multicellularity and a division of labor. Bioessays 27, 299â€“310 (2005) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2491">15</a></sup>. Five of these steps correlate with the evolution of cell groups<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Herron, M. D., Hackett, J. D., Aylward, F. O. &amp; Michod, R. E. Triassic origin and early radiation of multicellular volvocine algae. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 3254â€“3258 (2009) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2495">7</a></sup>, a period of rapid evolutionary change (tempo). This view emphasizes the importance, and subsequent modification, of innovations correlating with undifferentiated colonies (mode). Finding support for this view, we have generalized these 12 steps into three major phases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>): the evolution of cell cycle regulation to form cooperative groups via cellâ€“cell adhesion, the evolution of increased organismal size, and the evolution of differentiated germ and soma cells. Having sequenced the genome of <i>Gonium</i>, along with the published genomes of <i>Chlamydomonas</i> and <i>Volvox</i>, we can now identify the genetic pathways associated with each of these steps. The evolution of undifferentiated colonies correlates with <i>RB</i> cell cycle regulatory pathway evolution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig3">Figs 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig4">4</a>), which is further modified as complexity increases in the Volvocales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Ferris, P. J. et al. Evolution of an expanded sex-determining locus in Volvox. Science 328, 351â€“354 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2521">24</a></sup>. Increased organismal size toward <i>Volvox</i> correlates with an expansion of pherophorins and MMPs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>). Finally the evolution of the <i>regA</i> gene cluster underlies somatic differentiation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig5">Fig. 5a,b</a>). Future sequencing of additional Volvocales genomes should clarify the evolutionary steps required for the evolution of germ and soma. Our three-phase model for the emergence of multicellularity, supported by the genetic pathways important for their evolution, changes our understanding of the tempo and mode of multicellular evolution previously obscured in other taxa such as plants, fungi and animals due to genomic divergence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ncomms11370#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Conceptual model for the evolution of multicellularity."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Conceptual model for the evolution of multicellularity.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fncomms11370/MediaObjects/41467_2016_Article_BFncomms11370_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="165"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Multicellularity hinges on the evolution of cell cycle regulation in a multicellular context with subsequent evolution of cellular differentiation (here, cell size-based) and increased body size.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ncomms11370/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Interestingly, an emerging theme throughout the evolution of multicellularity is that the genetic basis for the evolutionary transition emerges much earlier than anticipated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Suga, H. et al. The Capsaspora genome reveals a complex unicellular prehistory of animals. Nat. Commun. 4, 2325â€“2333 (2013) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2563">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 1â€“9 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2566">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hanschen, E. R., Ferris, P. J. &amp; Michod, R. E. Early evolution of the genetic basis for soma in the Volvocaceae. Evolution 68, 2014â€“2025 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2569">32</a></sup>. In plants and animals, <i>RB</i> proteins are important for regulating both cell proliferation and differentiation by highly complex locus interactions with chromatin and chromatin remodelling factors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Sage, J. The retinoblastoma tumor suppressor and stem cell biology. Genes Dev. 26, 1409â€“1420 (2012) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2576">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kuwabara, A. &amp; Gruissem, W. Arabidopsis Retinoblastoma-related and Polycomb group proteins: Cooperation during plant cell differentiation and development. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 2667â€“2676 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2579">38</a></sup>. Our finding that the <i>RB</i> pathway was co-opted early for multicellularity in undifferentiated colonies suggests that the template for subsequent evolutionary innovations in developmental programs was laid out during the transition to undifferentiated multicellularity via <i>RB</i> and cell cycle modifications, rather than with emergence of germ and somatic cellular differentiation. Interestingly, <i>RB</i> has been further co-opted for a role in sexual differentiation in <i>Volvox</i>, where there are male- and female-specific isoforms of <i>RB</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Ferris, P. J. et al. Evolution of an expanded sex-determining locus in Volvox. Science 328, 351â€“354 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2598">24</a></sup>. This suggests that the evolution of multicellular cell cycle regulation was a critical step for the evolution of multicellularity. By comparing the genomes of these three volvocine green algae, we have determined that the mechanism of multicellular evolution is primarily co-option and regulatory modification of existing genetic pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kapheim, K. M. et al. Genomic signature of evolutionary transitions from solitary to group living. Science 348, 1139â€“1144 (2015) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2602">39</a></sup>. Gene duplication forms the basis of subsequent multicellular innovations.</p><p>The genomic age is illuminating the genetic pathways that are important for the evolution of multicellularity in other organisms where genes such as cadherins and integrins in animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Suga, H. et al. The Capsaspora genome reveals a complex unicellular prehistory of animals. Nat. Commun. 4, 2325â€“2333 (2013) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2609">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="King, N. et al. The genome of the choanoflagellate Monosiga brevicollis and the origin of metazoans. Nature 451, 783â€“788 (2008) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2612">4</a></sup> and cell wall biogenesis genes in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 1â€“9 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2616">6</a></sup>. These are roughly analogous to metalloproteases and pherophorins in the volvocine algae highlighting convergences on similar genetic innovations for multicellularity. The substantial innovation and expansion of transcription factors and signalling networks found in animals and plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Suga, H. et al. The Capsaspora genome reveals a complex unicellular prehistory of animals. Nat. Commun. 4, 2325â€“2333 (2013) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2620">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 1â€“9 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2623">6</a></sup> is not present in the volvocine algae. However, the volvocine algae demonstrate the critical role of transcriptional regulation of the cell cycle by <i>RB</i> for the formation of undifferentiated colonies. <i>RB</i> proteins regulate the cell cycle of most eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Olson, B. J. S. C. et al. Regulation of the Chlamydomonas cell cycle by a stable, chromatin-associated retinoblastoma tumor suppressor complex. Plant Cell 22, 3331â€“3347 (2010) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2634">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Khidr, L. &amp; Chen, P.-L. RB, the conductor that orchestrates life, death and differentiation. Oncogene 25, 5210â€“5219 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2637">26</a></sup>, and are tumour suppressors in humans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Khidr, L. &amp; Chen, P.-L. RB, the conductor that orchestrates life, death and differentiation. Oncogene 25, 5210â€“5219 (2006) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2641">26</a></sup>, suggesting a broader role for <i>RB</i> and cell cycle regulation during the evolution of multicellularity.</p><p>The implications of these findings are greater than simply identifying when genes evolved during the evolution of multicellularity. Theoretical work has emphasized the need for greater understanding of the origin of an integrated group life cycle during the evolution of multicellularity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Shelton, D. E. &amp; Michod, R. E. Group selection and group adaptation during a major evolutionary transition: insights from the evolution of multicellularity in the volvocine algae. Biol. Theory 9, 452â€“469 (2014) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2652">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Hanschen, E. R., Shelton, D. E. &amp; Michod, R. E. in Evolutionary transitions to multicellular life: principles and mechanisms eds Nedelcu A. M., Ruiz-Trillo I. 165â€“188Springer (2015) ." href="/articles/ncomms11370#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d3334e2655">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Libby, E. &amp; Rainey, P.
